When one of their own is found murdered, Rorschach, who has continued to fight crime despite the Keene Act, takes it upon himself to investigate the murder.  He finds signs that lead him to believe this murder will not be an isolated incident and warns his retired comrades while he continues his investigation.<br /><br />Watchmen is amazing.  Not only is it amazing as far as graphic novels go, it's better and more intelligently written than a lot of lauded literature that I've read.  Each section of the story is told from the point of view of a different character.  Each character thinks differently, so you get the story from several different perspectives.  In doing this, you begin to see that there is no clear right or wrong.  What's right from one character's perspective is wrong from another character's perspective.  You learn that not everything is black and white.  There are shades of gray and lots of them.  This is a book about government and politics; this is a book about philosophy; this is a book about science; but most of all, this is a book about humanity, its flaws, and its beauty.  I've never read anything like this before and it will stay with me for a very long time.

B006L4MWVE|5.0|Imagine this: a country is run by a totalitarian government, which occasionally selects groups of teenagers to methodically kill each other. On TV. Until only one remains.<br /><br />\\"The Hunger Games\\"? No, actually it's \\"Battle Royale,\\" a bloody and harrowing movie that came out several years earlier, depicting an alternate Japan where the youth are forced to kill each other. It's one of those movies that is strangely fascinating and filled with social commentary, with brilliant performances from pretty much everyone concerned.<br /><br />A class of high-schoolers are being transported on a bus, when they are gassed to unconsciousness. When they awake, they have electronic collars around their necks. A former teacher named Kitano explains that they have been chosen for the B.R. (\\"Battle Royale\\") Act, wherein teenagers are forced to fight each other to the death until only one is left standing.<br /><br />If you don't play, try to escape, refuse to kill or stray into a \\"death zone,\\" the collars explode.  Each teenager is given food, water and a random item, and are set loose on a remote island.<br /><br />It doesn't take long for them to fall prey to suicide, paranoia, bombs and one student's brilliant efforts to hack the system. As the teens slowly weed each other out, Shuya Nanahara and his girlfriend Noriko try to find a way out that doesn't involve death. But if they refuse to kill, then they must escape the fascist nightmare... which no one has done before.<br /><br />\\"Battle Royale\\" (both the novel and the movie) was condemned in Japan for being so violent, and having a bunch of normal high schoolers killing each other off. But \\"Battle Royale\\" would have been striking even if it hadn't been publicized like that -- not only is it viscerally fascinating, but it asks the question straight-out: how much will people do to survive? Who would you kill to live?<br /><br />It also seems to be a story about how governments will oppress innocent people in response to the brutality of a few. However, the movie avoids any outright preaching -- instead it uncoils a tense plot, peppered with bursts of bloody violence, sexual assault and murder. There's a wild, frenetic energy to the story, and director Kinji Fukasaku unleashes it through every cold, bloodspattered scene.<br /><br />Fun trivia: Fukasaku actually lived through something almost as cold-blooded during World War II, when he saw children having to hide from bombs under EACH OTHER. The survivors had to clear away the corpses.<br /><br />Tatsuya Fujiwara and Aki Maeda really give outstanding performances in this story as nice, normal, everyday kids like the ones who live down the street, but suddenly they're faced with their friends and classmates... wanting to kill them. Takeshi Kitano gives a bloodcurdlingly creepy performance as Kitano, who is nevertheless given plenty of fleshing-out (such as a fondness for one of the students).<br /><br />Violent, creepy and wonderfully atmospheric, \\"Battle Royale\\" is a brilliant cult movie that takes a look at how far people will go to stay alive. By strategically placing the Light Houses, one can divide the cleaning area into two sections and the pair together divides the cleaning area into 3 cleaning areas - to be cleaned in succession one after the other, the LHs signals for Roomba to systematically complete one area before allowing it to move into & clean the next area, and in turn the third area.  This way the cleaning is more effectively managed for better results.  When all the sections are complete, the LHs navigate the Roomba back to its charging base.  This is what is referred to as the \\"Room To Room Navigation\\" capability.  A very useful feature for the larger home.<br /><br />The Virtual wall OTOH, simply prohibits the Roomba from crossing to the other side.  This works to keep Roomba out of areas not requiring cleaning or the Scooba from your carpets or expensive rugs.<br /><br />My first run took 1 hour to clean 150sqf.  Today I deployed the LHs and Roomba effectively cleaned each section for 25mins each before returning to it's base. Beautiful navigation. The same upstairs.  I can't praise the 780 enough.  If you are deciding between the 770 and 780, and your home can be discretely sectioned, get the 780.  If you are in a studio apartment, then the Room to Room Navigation is uneccessary.<br /><br />Roombas 577 and 780 comes with a pair each.

B004N44SOI|5.0|I really loved The Heroin Diaries, and purchased this CD expecting more of the same. While it is undeniably the same band, TIGH is not quite as hard-edged. The title track is the clear-cut rocker-song on the album - a great way to open the CD. \\"Lies of the Beautiful People\\" - lyrically, this is obviously a theme that has done before, and done to death. Nikki likes to make it out like he's some philosophical genius, but this song's theme has actually been done to death. Still a very cool song, though! James Michael's vocals are simply amazing, very versatile! And DJ Ashba's guitar work is amazing as well. Track 3 - \\"Are You With Me Now\\" - is my personal favorite. I see a lot of reviews bagging on that song due to its commercial feel. That doesn't bother me. Who says a rock song can't have a catchy hook? Maybe it doesn't sound like some genre-breaking masterpiece, but so what? The lyrics are easy to relate to, and the chorus is super catchy. The only criticism I have is the track \\"Sure Feels Right\\". It's a very cool, mellow tune, but has a definite country-music overtone to it, and doesn't seem suited to this band or the CD. It does demonstrate the band's versatility, but it's kind of a misfit song in the midst of the others.<br /><br />The ballad that ends the album, \\"Skin\\", is getting a lot of attention. I have the same problem with this song as I have with \\"Lies of the Beautiful People\\". It's a good song, but the topic has been done to death before, and it's not that ground-breaking or profound piece that so many people are making it out to be. The piano is wonderful, though, and James's vocals, as always, are phenomenal.<br /><br />Honestly, I was kind of so-so about the album when I first listened to it, but after repeated listens, it really grew on me. I enjoy listening to every song on here now. I have definite favorites, but I no longer skip through it.<br /><br />Don't buy this album if you're expecting a hard-rocking ride all the way through. But there's a little something for everyone in here - those who can appreciate different genres and types of music will really enjoy this. Those close-minded \\"Metal or die\\" types who think that if music doesn't rock hard, it sucks...you won't enjoy this. Personally, I appreciate diversity in my music, so Sixx:AM gets 5 stars from me :)n here - those who can appreciate different genres and types of music will really enjoy this. Those close-minded \\"Metal or die\\" types who think that if music doesn't rock hard, it sucks...you won't enjoy this. B0050DIWHU|5.0|Even as someone who has committed to an academic biomedical path for the foreseeable future, who you might not expect to be within the book's intended audience, I found the material in this book to be quite useful. It is strongest in discussing how to develop mutually enriching relationships and how to tap into the resources of your network to help achieve your goals. This is unsurprising because Reid started one of the most robust online social networks, and Ben, as evidenced by his blog and connections, is a networking maven. So, I'd recommend it especially to people who want to improve in these areas. I'd also recommend it to people who are somewhat skeptical that networking matters, because this book might very well help convince you that it does.  Here are a few of the other particularly interesting themes:  1) In part of one chapter they discuss the idea of an internal review, wherein you tally up your hard assets (e.g., cash, holdings) as well as your soft assets (e.g., skills, experiences, and connections). For the soft assets, this involves the use of a sort of \\"Bayesian evidence\\", which means that you must not only consider the evidence that you are strong in an area but also the evidence that you are weak in it. Now, just like a company doesn't tell its competitors what its weak points are, you probably wouldn't want to tell a potential employer that you are weak in an area. But, it is useful to be honest with yourself about your relative strengths and weaknesses, because it can help you allocate your personal investments in more optimal ways. This is a topic in which their \\"think of your career like an entrepreneur thinks of his/her company\\" analogy has tremendous power.  2) The book devotes some time to arguing against the idea that you have a true self with one true passion, which you need to devote time and energy to \\"discovering\\". In reality we all have many selves, many possible selves, many possible passions, and many possible routes to achieve ouraspirations. Ben and Reid tap into this framework in their explanation of the \\"almost ready, aim, fire, aim, fire\\" career strategy. They discuss how the most useful way to learn whether you like something is not to think about it but to simply try it, in as low cost of a way as possible. This reminded me of both Charlie Hoehn's \\"Recession Proof Graduate\\" and Cal Newport's \\"Career Craftsman Manifesto.\\" The strategy espoused in this book relates to both of those strategies in a complementary way.  3) One thing that you will notice when reading the book is that it often references LinkedIn. (Just to be clear, this isn't *all* that common--using the wonderful Kindle search application and filtering out the times where Reid is discussing his own career, I count 24 such instances.) Some might see this as \\"propaganda\\", but I actually liked it and found it useful. There is a trade-off between being giving abstract advice, which could potentially be relevant for decades but is more difficult to know how to implement, and giving actionable advice, which has a shorter shelf-life but is easier to know how to employ. Much of the advice in this book is on the abstract side, but the references to LinkedIn (as well as to other contemporary tools like Twitter and Facebook) help ground the book's advice in today's real world.

B003Y5HWJU|1.0|I look at the favourable reviews for this program and wonder how, or why, the reviewers have not noticed:  (a) that the script-writing is awful.  Where to begin?  The ridiculous liberties taken with history would be a start.  The implausible dialogue would be a close second.  Did nobody read the script aloud before they started filming?  Did nobody do any study of how people spoke at the time?  The two dimensional character development would be third. Characters cut from cardboard, stuck together with wall-paper paste.  (b) the acting is just bad.  It would be bad even for a soap opera.  \\"Wooden\\" is an appropriate adjective for much of it.  Then there are the ever-painful attempts by latter-day actors to mimick accents.  If you have no idea what an Irish accent sounds like, don't try to do an Irish accent.  If you don't have any idea what an Italian accent ought to sound like, don't do an Italian accent.  If you don't do Polish, or Yiddish, or German, then leave it alone.  (c)  the camera-work is bad in a way that is difficult to explain.  It is the kind of camera work that might be ok in an airplane safety video explaining how to buckle your lap belt, how to find the life vest, and how to proceed to the emergency exit.  That anybody can find work in TV drama with this kind of camera-work is a very sad comment about TV.  (d)  the \\"no-name brand\\" camera work may flow from the direction, which, not surprisingly, is awful.  There is nothing clever in this production.  Nothing.  It includes the same gratuitous violence, unrealistic, over-the-top, \\"violence-as-pornography\\" you would expect.  The direction can never, ever, leave the viewer to figure out anything by himself or herself.  Heaven forbid that any scene should have any (intentional) irony, or anything but the most obvious, leaden double-entendre.  That is why the camera is positioned and operated as if they are trying to make an instructional video, so that the viewer can't possibly misunderstand what the director is trying to get across.  It is why the dialogue is wrong: Always use contemporary 2011 dialogue, and, if in doubt, make it stilted, and throw in lots of 2011-style swearing, because there is a chance that if the script required dialogue from the 1920's, spoken as it might actually have been, the viewers wouldn't understand - they might need sub-titles, and the intended audience for this show isn't the kind that knows what to do with sub-titles.  Use every possible cliche or worn phrase, to make sure that every possible viewer, no matter how stupid, will understand them. The production seems to be driven by the principle: Why do anything subtle, when you can hit the viewer over the head with it ten times instead.  It is why the violence can't be just ordinary violence, it has to be cartoon violence, pornographic violence, with large quantities of blood splattered on everything in the scene - otherwise the audience might not understand that they really, really, really killed the man. Both the violence and the ridiculous sex are over-acted in the manner of slapstick comedy, or vaudeville, or silent films before talkies.  It's a cartoon caricature.  It is clumsy, ham-fisted, bull-in-a-china-shop, second or third-grade work.  When the three stooges did this kind of stuff, it was intended to be funny.  This production wasn't intended to be funny. It isn't.  It is just bad.  If there is one word that sums up the multitude of failings of this production it would be: \\"Artless\\".  The only things about this series that are reasonably well done?  Somebody has paid attention to costumes, furniture, and vintage cars; somebody else knew a fair bit about music.

B0053YGYO4|4.0|You'll hear many complaints - mostly from long-time Coldplay fans and/or fans of their older music - about the group's newest CD, Mylo Xyloto. The reason being the obvious change between the 4 main older CD's and their one newest.  In the shortest explanation possible, what many fail to understand is this: The artist who keeps the same exact style for their whole career may be safe from the possibility of persecution, but their work is bound to get old sooner or later.  Coming from a listener of Coldplay since their first album, I am extremely inspired by Coldplay's ability to take a leap and bring about change that I'm sure they knew would face disapproval. What they've done is something many others fail to succeed in achieving.  Now onto the actual album.  All of the songs successfully include a similar style, although at the same time they all sound different. They transition very well, and all have a unique tune that hasn't been reused and recycled by many of the cookie cutter artists you hear today.  One song may have an acoustic feel about it (such as the obvious choice, U.F.O), while another may have a poppish-sounding feel (such as Princess of China).  A lot of people dislike Princess of China, for the reason that it's a collaboration with Rihanna. I can see why many wouldn't like this song; I mean, it's Rihanna, a pop artist, playing with Coldplay, a soft-rock band. Nonetheless, I like it all the same.  The one problem I've found is that about five to four of the songs could've had the vocals louder. For instance the second-to-last track, Don't Let it Break Your Heart, has very soft vocals. Regardless of their intentions, Chris's wonderful voice deserves to be equal with all the other instruments.  So although the low volume of their singer's voice has taken away a star, this album has helped me realize that, whether it be small or large, sometimes change can be a great help.  ~ Lilian

Complicating Bryce's mission are \\"the Feather Men\\" a group of former SAS members who protect their own. Their enforcer Spike Logan (Clive Owen) is sent to investigate and prevent the killing of its members.<br /><br />A well made thriller from first time feature director Gary McKendry, \\"Killer Elit\\" positively bristles with energy and features something a bit unusual for a thriller of this type--actual character development. I suspect that those complaining about the exposition don't care for those more paranoid spy thrillers from another era.<br /><br />The one flaw with the film is that it could have been trimmed a bit but that's a minor complaint at best. B004J4WN0I|4.0|Atticus O'Sullivan is a centuries old Druid who looks like a 22-year old living in Tempe, Arizona. He runs a store in the college town and lives with his Irish wolf-hound, Oberon (yes, he's named after Shakespeare's fairy king). He's living a fairly quiet existence given he's been hunted by an angry god for at least 1,000 years but has been pretty safe since he decided to make his home in Arizona.<br /><br />Now it seems his good fortune has run out as Aengus d'Og has found him and is sending emissaries to try their hand at offing him. The story doesn't lack in imagination as the assortment of foes is creative and diverse. This is Celtic mythology at its finest with Atticus in the center of gods and goddesses behaving badly, using mortals, witches and Fae to fight their political battles. The dialogue is pithy and some of the best is between Atticus and Oberon. It's impossible to know who is telling the truth, bending it a little or just flat out lying and it makes the story delicious. After awhile, I decided to just go along for the ride, enjoying some of the best writing I've read in this genre. Atticus is so interesting and his appeal is hard to define but it's there. All of the secondary characters are very well defined, provide their own intrigue and add effectively to the tale.<br /><br />I was reluctant to start this book as it didn't seem to fit my interest and now I'm thrilled that it was put in front of me by friends whose opinions I trust. This is one of the best stories I've read this year and I'm looking forward to the rest of the series.

1594744769|5.0|Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children is an entertaining, different and very well-put-together novel and an all-around great introduction to a new author. The story is a sort-of Science Fiction/Horror/Fantasy all rolled into one and is told to great effect using mostly authentic old photographs to supplement the story by providing amazing visuals of the \\"peculiar\\" people and events of the story. It makes me wonder if the author created his story to fit the photos that he and other collectors had found or if he wrote his story and found the right pictures to fit (or some combination of the two.) Either way it made for a fascinating and fast-paced novel that I found extremely difficult to put down and I eagerly await a sequel so that I can further follow the adventures of these \\"Peculiar Children.\\"<br /><br />In terms of style, the main character and feel of the book were a bit reminiscent of Joe Hill's recent novel Horns and the style of the book did bring to mind Brian Selznick's The Invention of Hugo Cabret. However the book itself is not in any way a copy of these two works or any other. It is a wonderfully unique story and presentation that brings the characters to life in a way that most other novels do not.

You'll be disappointed cause Mangini plays differently, and you'll focus so much on the drums that you won't enjoy all the beautiful music this album has to offer. It was very hard to me, as a drummer, to get rid of Portnoy's drumming style, but I managed to do it, and I since loved the album. LaBrie's singing is wonderful and some of the guitar riffs are just awesome, super technical. I felt the same with this album, trying to figure out the time signatures and technical aspects of most of the songs. And I'm still trying to figure then out! B003XF1XOQ|5.0|As the book progressed I had to lay it down several times, hands shaking, heart breaking, and tears flowing. While many books has left me angry, sad, shocked, and made me throw the book across the room, Mockingjay is worse. I felt so much for the characters that the ending completely killed me. It's a difficult book to read--in more ways than one.<br /><br />While we finally get the rebellion that we have been looking forward to from the first book, it's not the glorious uprising with Katniss as the heroic leader that we expected. Because while Katniss story begins with a brave heroine sacrificing herself for her sister and overcoming obstacles, paired with a romantic love story and the hope of rebellion, it is in the end a tragedy of war. Collins paint a bleak and dystopian picture, at time almost seems to revel in the horrors--reaching an almost unbearable level at the end.<br /><br />The people of the rebellion--as well as the readers--continue to pile expectations on Katniss, the girl on fire, the face of the rebellion. But in the end she's just a seventeen year old girl, and while she is stronger than many around her, she breaks under the weight. The girl on fire is doused in the horrors of war and the fire put out; the symbolism is painfully fitting.<br /><br />The romantic plot that is so important in the previous books might seem like it's washed away in the rebellion, but it's still there--only in the guise of love. Katniss' detached personality become painfully apparent both to Peeta and to Katniss herself, but the love remains, and the fact that it's still there during the last pages of the book shows us how strong it is.<br /><br />As the lines between who is good and evil become blurred, it's Katniss who provides the moral compass throughout the chaos. I always thought one of Katniss' important strengths was her compassion, which stayed with her through so many hardships that would have burned it out for other people. Like Gale. Unlike a lot of people, I thought Gale's story felt natural, and the last breaking point between him and Katniss didn't come as a shocking surprise. He changed during the war, as his fire for revenge consumed his compassion. He is unmoved in collapsing a mountain on his enemies in an event that is comparable to the mine accidents that killed both his and Katniss' fathers, and his guilt doesn't catch up to him until one of his creations directly affects Katniss. The guilt combined with Katniss' passive accusation finally drove him away.<br /><br />I guess what I really loved about the book was all the subtlety. The writing is fast and short, and it leaves a lot up for interpretation. This together with the symbolism and foreshadowing will make me read the books again and again. Just like good, well crafted books should.<br /><br />I think it's fitting that although we can replace lost limbs and heal burned skin, what we can never heal is a broken soul. That is what the epilogue is so painful to me. Katniss and Peeta will never be the same, the people they were before the Hunger Games. This, more than everything, is the point of the series for me. \\"It takes ten times as long to put yourself back together as it does to fall apart.\\"<br /><br />Although it made me cry like nothing before, I thought the end was fitting. I hated it--I just wanted everyone to be happy--but I don't think it could have turned out any other way after what happens in the books. When I finished the book I was a drained wreck, and reading discussions about the book was almost like therapy. It made me make sense of why things happened the way it did. Contemplating the book has helped overcome the grief, making sense of all the things happening.<br /><br />I could tell you what I think happened, but some walks you have to take alone.the last breaking point between him and Katniss didn't come as a shocking surprise. 0321701763|1.0|Despite being designated as the 'official training workbook' for Adobe Photoshop CS5, this book is perhaps the best example of how NOT to write an instructional text on Photoshop CS5. The biggest fault in 'Classroom in a Book' is with the method in which material is presented. This book is neither a detailed primer on Photoshop tools/techniques (like Martin Evening's books), nor a practical guide on real-world post-processing or retouching techniques (like Scott Kelby's books).<br /><br />Like Kelby's book, you'll find that this book is mostly structured as a set of very prescriptive step-by-step instructions to perform a specific task. However, the exercises in this book are often contrived, and in many cases lack practical value. In fact, instead of being a training manual, this book feels more like a laboratory guide for beta-testers that are being asked to tour through specific features of the product.<br /><br />You will notice this style right from the very beginning. For example, many example exercises have the reader start Photoshop holding down the special control sequence to clear settings (and this instruction is repeated over and over throughout the book). In addition, instead of just focusing on techniques designed for one audience, there is somewhat random selection of techniques covered which vary from those useful to digital photographers, to those used by designers or graphic artists. Sadly, there isn't enough depth for any one of those audiences to really learn enough to be useful.<br /><br />Those new to Photoshop (which this book tries to cater towards), will likely come away very frustrated with not only the poor organization of material in the book, but also the frequent lack of satisfactory explanations of what particular tools/techniques actually do. For example, as early as page 21, the book walks you through creating a curves adjustment layer (which really deserves its own chapter!) and just tells the reader to click on specific icons and enter specific values and 'notice the results', without explaining what really happened.<br /><br />At best, readers of this book will only be left with a superficial idea of what Photoshop CS5 is really capable of, and no real understanding of how or why to use particular tools/techniques. It is really baffling to me who would find value in a book like this. Neither inspiring, nor educational, this book fails spectacularly in educating the reader on how to use this very complex product. Not recommended.ce the results', without explaining what really happened. 0385535139|5.0|John Grisham has written many good books, a couple of terrible clunkers, and a few superb ones.  \\"THE LITIGATORS\\", his latest, is the best of them all.  It's about a young lawyer's escape from his soul-destroying job at a white-shoe law firm, and how he ends up working with a couple of ambulance-chasers called Finney & Figg.  Grisham's ironic wit and unerring sense of the ridiculous guides the reader through some hilarious adventures, as the unlikely trio take on a hopeless product liability lawsuit and get themselves in more trouble than can be imagined.  So skillfully are the characters portrayed, that they soon step out of the book and into our lives as real people whose troubles and triumphs become our own. Even as we smile at their foibles, we become genuinely concerned about them, and wonder how they're doing long after we turn the last page.  Borrow it if you can, but I suggest you run right out and buy it, so you can re-read some of its memorable passages whenever you need a chuckle.

0307956350|4.0|This is a traditional spy thriller with a twist (which is, I'm sure, how it was sold to the publisher). The protagonist is female and her \\"cover\\" is that she's a suburban mother and housewife. Or is she a suburban stay-at-home mom that also happens to be a spy? (You can also throw in the tired trope of \\"getting out of the game,\\" only to be \\"pulled back in.\\")<br /><br />This is a fast-paced, basically well-written tale that moves at breakneck speed. I think the ideas--an overlay of the marriage dynamic on a traditional spy tale, with the added intrigue of a James Bond gender flip-flop--are better than the execution. Like many books of this genre, not all of the twists, red herrings and double and triple agent reveals make total sense when taken whole, and the spy cliches comes at you fast and furious, but it's a still a rollicking ride that will keep you guessing until the end.<br /><br />The first half, which deals with more domestic drama than international intrigue, is better-written than the second, which gets more and more far-fetched as the plot twists ratchet their way up to utterly unbelievable.<br /><br />My biggest turn off was the stiff dialogue and a few too many woman-in-peril \\"jokes\\" where Kate, the supposed super-agent, is shown to be, \\"just a dumb girl,\\" something the author apparently find hilarious, since he uses the ploy again and again. For that sin, I almost gave this one a mere three stars, but, in the end, despite the weaknesses I found myself entertained. (I so frequently wish Amazon had a 1/2-star rating option, but I digress ...)<br /><br />Certainly not a genre classic, this one clearly cribs elements from a LOT of better spy stories, but it's still pretty fun. A good one when you're in the mood for some mindless fluff.

Poppy Wyatt never realized how essential her phone was until she lost it...on the very day that she happened to lose the heirloom emerald engagement ring that her brilliant fiance, Magnus, gave her soon after their relationship began.  Poppy can't bear the thought of living life without her lifeline and feels as if a miracle has dropped into her lap when she finds an abandoned phone in the trash.  When no one arrives immediately to claim it, Poppy engages the \\"finders keepers\\" rule and adopts it as her own.  Unfortunately, the owner Sam Roxton, doesn't agree.  Poppy uses all of her powers of persuasion to get him to reluctantly allow her to \\"share\\" the phone while she waits for news about her missing engagement ring.  As the days draw closer to Poppy's upcoming wedding, the two develop a complicated relationship of shared texts, emails and voice mails that reveal interesting aspects of their characters and lives and - seeing themselves through each others' eyes - makes them realize that their lives may not be as perfect as they once thought.  Are they each making huge mistakes in the way they deal with other people?  Sam can't understand why Poppy allows Magnus' academic family to intimidate her while Poppy grows more and more frustrated with his refusal to allow people to get close to him. 0375842209|4.0|~4.5/5<br />I'd heard about this book before, but never what it was about. I'd heard that it starts weird, and that it was dark, or something. But I didn't know until we started it in book club what it was actually about. And that the only reason it was weird is because it's narrated by Death. Which I don't really think is all that weird.<br />It's set during World War 2, which made me a little wary. I've only read and seen a few things set during that time, and I'm usually not a big fan of war or historical novels. But this one was different. What with them being Germans, and secretly not liking how things are going. Not actively fighting (for the most part), just living their lives. And hiding a Jew in their basement. The main thing that caught my attention, though, is that it's told by Death.<br />Death is one of my favorite things about this story, and how well he was done. Writing this book from Death's point-of-view was kind of a brilliant idea on Zusak's part. He's his own character, cynical and haunted but also unconditionally interested in humans, and he knows everything. I really liked how he was written, and how he would go forward, and warn you about things or tell you how things with certain characters would turn out, and then go back to the present with Liesel. Death is a person, just like everyone else in this story, and he doesn't kill anyone, just carries them to wherever they go after. The way it was told reminded me a bit of Mary & Max, a claymation movie that I saw recently that was also really good (I would suggest it).<br />And all of the characters were really well done. There's Liesel, the mostly main character, who's somewhat innocent but fun, and who loves to read and kind of 'steals' books (only not how you would think), and who cares and is smart and tough and a little impulsive. And there's Rudy, who is adventurous and impulsive and athletic and loves Liesel, and who I also kind of love. And Liesel's Papa, who's so sweet to Liesel and genuine and nice and not a Nazi. And then there's Liesel's Mama, who is crude and curses at everyone but still loves them and cares. Oh, and Max, who is Jewish and so has to hide, and is afraid but gets close to Liesel and makes her these sweet books, with perfectly amateurish artwork. There are so many different characters, and they all have their own story and personality, and they are all great. And I just love them all.<br />And the end, particularly the scenes between Liesel and her Papa, and Liesel and Rudy, are so sweet and overwhelmingly sad. I felt like crying at them (and I am not a crier). But I guess I should have suspected that, what with the subject matter and all. I still didn't want them to happen, though.<br />There is so much to this book. It's not a small book, or in any way a quick read, especially because of the length, but also because there is just so much to it. It's a little subtle, and cynical, and you know that nothing really good is going to happen, but it's not a depressing book. It's...yea, sad, but also realistic, and honest, and imaginative, creative. So many things. And it's so good.<br />It's just a great book, in many ways. And I see why other people think it's amazing, because it is.<br />(Sidenote: Also, my copy of the book, that I got used, someone had spilt some kind of liquid on, making the first quarter of the pages crinkly and colored. And, while however sad and terrible that is, I felt it was a little fitting.)nd not a Nazi. And then there's Liesel's Mama, who is crude and curses at everyone but still loves them and cares. Oh, and Max, who is Jewish and so has to hide, and is afraid but gets close to Liesel and makes her these sweet books, with perfectly amateurish artwork. There are so many different characters, and they all have their own story and personality, and they are all great. And I just love them all.  And the end, particularly the scenes between Liesel and her Papa, and Liesel and Rudy, are so sweet and overwhelmingly sad. I felt like crying at them (and I am not a crier). But I guess I should have suspected that, what with the subject matter and all. I still didn't want them to happen, though.  There is so much to this book. It's not a small book, or in any way a quick read, especially because of the length, but also because there is just so much to it. It's a little subtle, and cynical, and you know that nothing really good is going to happen, but it's not a depressing book. It's...yea, sad, but also realistic, and honest, and imaginative, creative. So many things. And it's so good.  It's just a great book, in many ways. B001IBIHQ4|4.0|August 1942. The United States Marine Corps lands in force on the island of Guadalcanal and begins the long, gruelling campaign of 'island-hopping'. Over the next three years they will take the fight against Japanese forces on islands all across the western Pacific, drawing ever closer to the Japanese home islands and victory.<br /><br />The Pacific is HBO's companion series to their acclaimed 2001 series Band of Brothers. Like the earlier series, The Pacific follows American soldiers across a single theatre of war (albeit a far vaster one). Unlike Band of Brothers, which was able to concentrate on a single company of the 10st Airborne and cover its journey in some detail (the whole series, bar the opening training sequence, only spanned nine months), The Pacific is rather diffuse in its focus. It covers a period of three years and the almost modular nature of the war in the Pacific (multiple major battles raging simultaneously on islands hundreds of miles apart) means it is impossible to focus on one unit throughout the whole war. The series copes with the problem by dividing the narrative into three storylines following three marines (John Basilone, Bob Leckie and Eugene Sledge), but this ultimately proves to be a move with mixed success.<br /><br />Plaudits first. The Pacific is visually stunning. Filmed on location on Australia's Pacific coast, the series employs impressive location filming, practical special effects and CGI enhancements to depict WWII combat on-screen more viscerally and in a more convincing manner even than Band of Brothers. The Pacific really does look and sound like a movie, especially on Blu-Ray, and as an audio/visual experience is always impressive. The lead actors - Joseph Mazzello, James Badge Dale and Jon Seda as Sledge, Leckie and Basilone respectively - are impressive and are lent able support by the likes of William Sadler (as the infamous Colonel 'Chesty' Puller) and Rami Malek, who gives an offbeat but fascinating performance as 'Snafu' Shelton. In terms of direction, production values and acting, the series is overwhelmingly impressive.<br /><br />The issues arise from structural choices for the series. The Pacific War was a very different beast to the Western Front of WWII in Europe, spanning vaster distances and featuring the marines, navy and army fighting simultaneously on different fronts. An immediate drawback of only having ten episodes to tell this story is that other units and services outside the Marine Corps get short shrift. None of the immense naval battles in the theatre (Midway, the Coral Sea etc) are really mentioned and the massive American bombing campaign against Japan is not featured. There are some nods to the other services (the Battle of Savo Island is briefly watched by the marines from the shore; aerial assaults on Japanese positions are occasionally shown) but the focus is squarely on the marines.<br /><br />Even this is problematic. To get across the scale of the conflict, HBO conflates three separate stories together. John Basilone's story allows them to show life on the home front (Basilone was sent home after Guadalcanal, initially serving as a PR representative to raise money for the war and later as a marine instructor before returning to the field during the battle for Iwo Jima), whilst Bob Leckie is used to cover the initial stages of the fighting and also the role of Australia in the conflict. After Leckie is wounded and forced to return home, Eugene Sledge steps up as our principal POV character and we see most of the latter stages of the war through his eyes. This baton-passing approach to the narrative sounds good but ultimately feels disjointed, as the three soldiers' paths never cross (aside a fictitious brief meeting between Sledge and Leckie). It also complicates things by giving us three different sets of supporting characters to also get to know, which is a tall order for just ten episodes.<br /><br />Which isn't to say that what we are left withis still not compelling. The actors do a great job of selling their characters' respective storylines: Leckie is left on the front lines a little too long and begins suffering from battle fatigue; Basilone is tired of being hailed a hero at home whilst his friends are still fighting and is anxious to return to the front; and Sledge is angry at missing out the start of the war due to poor health and is keen to prove himself, only to ultimately start losing perspective as the gruelling months of combat unfold. These are familiar storylines, but are given added depth and weight by being true (all three marines are real, and Leckie and Sledge's memoirs provide the basis for the series).<br /><br />The overwhelming feeling of the series is one of despair. The fighting is savage and without quarter, and the marines often feel that their fighting and dying over specks of land in the middle of nowhere is pointless. There is a lack of any type of grand strategic overview which is true to the experience of the soldiers on the ground. Notably, there is no equivalent to the Why We Fight episode of Band of Brothers (where the 101st liberates a concentration camp and their horrific experiences harden their resolve to help destroy the Nazi regime). The marines are left feeling bereft of a purpose, which makes the horrors they experience strike home even harder. It's no wonder that many of them are shown breaking down or needing urgent time away from the front to reorient themselves. HBO should be congratulated for not downplaying these angles, but it often makes for very hard viewing.<br /><br />The Pacific (****) is ultimately worth watching. It's a stunning production that makes some hard comments about war and its ultimate savagery, even when a war is worth fighting. It doesn't glorify war and shows its horrors in full force, which can make for hard viewing, but the superior acting and writing make it compelling. Structurally the series is disjointed, and would have benefitted from a tighter focus on maybe just one or two of the marines featured. Extending it to three stretches the story almost to breaking point. However, once you adjust to the structural issues the series is an impressive piece of work. It is available now on DVD (UK, USA) and Blu-Ray (UK, USA).of selling their characters' respective storylines: Leckie is left on the front lines a little too long and begins suffering from battle fatigue; Basilone is tired of being hailed a hero at home whilst his friends are still fighting and is anxious to return to the front; and Sledge is angry at missing out the start of the war due to poor health and is keen to prove himself, only to ultimately start losing perspective as the gruelling months of combat unfold. B004CGUC06|4.0|Okay, I'll admit right off that I am not a Fellini fanatic.  I haven't watched his work in the past with a critical eye, nor have I made a study of his habits, tendencies, world-view, etc.  So I watched AMARCORD with essentially fresh eyes, knowing only that it was considered enough of a world classic to be presented on Bluray by Criterion.  I'm not sure I understand why it's a classic.  But it certainly was enjoyable.  Full of unbridled energy, unconcerned with narrative coherence and clearly dedicated to celebrating naughty fun, AMARCORD is a visual feast.  Allegedly, Fellini said \\"Amarcord\\" means \\"I remember\\"...but it does not.  Much like the film feels very much like a recollection a childhood gone by...but clearly is NOT realistic.  It is set in an Italian coastal village during the early fascist era (before WWII).  We basically follow one year in the life of this little town.  The camera focuses primarily on one extended family, but also visits with several other characters.  I hesitate to use the word \\"character\\" since what most of the citizens of this town are is \\"caricatures.\\"  Each actor is a visual extreme.  Whether the irate father who yells far more than is realistic (and has a HUGE mole on his bald head), or the garishly dressed town trollop...no one in the film rings quite true.  The best I can describe it is:  imagine a full grown man remembering the town of his youth, but using the world-view and libido of a teen aged boy who is simply dying to lose his virginity.  AMARCORD is full of fart jokes, crude sex jokes, and has a teen's fixation on large breasts and general mischief.  Apparently, this village was just a swirling mass of horny people!  This is not to say that Fellini has made an art house AMERICAN PIE...the movie is too cunning to be dismissed so simply.  It is so evocative of a \\"simpler\\" time...when bonfires were held in the village square to celebrate the arrival of spring, when the whole town puts to sea in rowboats with the hope of spotting a giant cruise ship coming by, when the church ran the moral show and when fascism was still mostly harmless pomp & circumstance.  We feel the pride of the people in their town and in their country (still less than 100 years old at the time).  As we follow the main characters around, we get to know them at small family gatherings, at major occasions, at weddings, at funerals, at school & work.  The entire movie is a string of incidents, only held together because we're seeing the same people over and over.  It's like we've been invited as guests for the year and our casual wanderings through the village sometimes take us to momentous happenings and other times to trivial scenes.  And occasionally, a \\"professorial\\" character will ride on his bike into our view and speak directly to us...just as though we were tourists who needed a little perspective.  AMARCORD simply does a fabulous job of capturing a time and place, as well as the character of that time & place.  It rambles often, but never runs out of steam.  It has great forward momentum all the time, and you never know what's going to happen next because it's a series of sketches.  Visually, it's quite stunning (and the BluRay is awesome) and it features some great music.  The performances are quite unrealistic, but they are all very consistent with the tone Fellini is clearly aiming for.  The movie feels utterly loose and random...yet it is clearly under the director's complete control.  To me, the \\"classic\\" label is a bit puzzling.  It's a well-made film, but seems like such a trifle too.  I don't really glean a \\"greater meaning \\" from it, nor do I find it to make some universal statement.  It's too garish and specific for that.  I give the film very high marks for achieving its own peculiar aims so well...but can't quite put it into the pantheon of 5-star films either.  The Criterion edition is stellar (although the commentary and included essay don't really enlighten as to the meaning of the film...theyfocus more on Fellini, and how his life compares to the life on the film, and how the film fits his overall body of work).  The video is superb and the sound is excellent.  Subtitles are a little better than usual (sometimes Criterion has shockingly bad subtitles.)
1439102813|5.0|This is the story of Alice Howland, a brilliant 50 year old cognitive psychology Harvard professor.  She has published articles and lectured around the world, the wife of John, a successful scientist and together they have 3 children.  An attorney married to an attorney, Tom a college student and Lydia is an actress.<br /><br />Alice begins to experience moments of forgetting and confusion which she chalks up to perhaps being from menopause, aging, her busy professional life, too much stress, not enough sleep etc.  Some of these moments include searching for hours for her Blackberry charger which is always plugged in the electrical socket right beside the bed, and a jog in Harvard Square which she is so familiar with, she suddenly becomes disoriented and now can't find her way back home.  Her memory comes back and she returns home.<br /><br />She was invited to be a guest speaker at Stanford to kick off the cognitive psychology fall colloquium series. B006IW8E0W|5.0|\\"Pina\\" (106 min.) is a thrilling documentary about/celebration of choreographer Pina Bausch's work. It is important to note that Bausch herself was involved in the preparations of this movie, but she passed away just before shooting beagan after a short but devastating bout with cancer. Director Wim Wenders halted the production, but the dancers of the Wuppertal Tanztheatre convinced him to carry on and to make the movie as a tribute to Bausch. And that it certainly is.<br /><br />The movie showcases 4 major pieces by Bausch, starting with The Rite of Spring, in which the dancers perform on a thick layer of dark sand. The other pieces are Cafe Mueller, Kontakthoff, and Vollmond. The latter is truly remarkable: there is a huge rock on the stage, and a continuous waterfall onto the stage, which eventually gets flooded for the most part. The dancers splish and splash their way to, through and onto it and it is an incredible visual experience. A number of scenes are also filmed in and around Wuppertal, including on the Wuppertal Schwebebahn (the \\"hanging monorail\\") as well as a number of industrial sites that are visually very powerful. The documentary includes short interviews with various company dancers on how Pina influenced them. Sadly, the are only snippets of old footage of Pina Bausch herself.<br /><br />I saw this in the theatre in 3D and while the 3D was good, I will say that the 3D aspect is not essential to appreciate this movie for what it is: a love note to modern ballet and to Pina Bausch. If you have any interest in ballet, you truly do not want to miss out on this movie. \\"Pina\\" was nominated for an Oscar for best documentary, and it is easy to see why. Highly, highly recommended!

B000SEGCRS|4.0|I'm just starting out in photography and it's by no means my profession.  I'm just a photo-junkie who finally decided to call it a hobby.  So I figured I might as well get a few books that could teach me how to get the most out of my fancy new digital SLR.  This book was at the top of most \\"top photography book\\" lists that I've found online, as it should be.  It's a really great starter-book and by that, I mean that it's non-technical and beginner-friendly.  This is both a good and a bad thing, but more on that later.  The author does a great job of spreading some light humor throughout the book and, while this was probably designed to make the book seem less intimidating, I found that it actually \\"swung the pendulum too far in the other direction,\\" so to speak.  Yes, I thought his joke about TACK SHARP being an acronym was funny, but now I'm not 100% convinced that I can trust his tip of referring to lenses as \\"glass\\" in photography stores (as in, \\"Don't ask for a new lens.  Say you need some new glass\\").  But that being said, his jokes and non-technical jargon made this very light and I was able to finish it in one sitting.  Now for the cons.  I say this book is \\"non-technical,\\" but that's actually not true.  It's actually at that awkward phase between total beginner and professional photographer.  He'll start name-dropping different terms and settings that you should set your camera to (i.e. aperture, ISO, etc) but never actually explains what they are.  He'll say that ISO is similar to film speed on the old film cameras, but that's about as much detail as you'll get.  So if you're a total, green-horned beginner, don't count on this being the only photography book on your shelf.  The other side of the coin is that by doing this, I'd imagine it would motivate someone to go out and get another book that goes into more detail.  The author does actually say that he hopes this book will inspire his readers to go out and learn more about photography.  So like I said, don't count on this being the only photography book you reference.  This is a really handy guide for beginner and intermediate photographers.  It provides some really great tips on everything from landscape and floral photography to shooting professional weddings and sporting events. And, because it shows you how to take great quality photos, the reader won't become frustrated and give up photography all together.  Keep in mind that this is a really short book, though, and by no means encompasses all there is to know about photography.  For something like that, I'd recommend turning to Complete Digital Photography by Ben Long.  That's a much more in-depth book...well it's really a photography textbook...but that can get you going on the technical side of photography.  In the meantime, I'd highly recommend The Digital Photography Book as a excellent source of information to get you started!

B004GTQ4JC|5.0|This is not really a sequel (though some story arcs from the original do get developed here), but a 2-part special that is inspired in part by Cranford, and also two other stories by author Elizabeth Gaskell, i.e. \\"The Moorland Cottage\\", and also \\"The Cage at Cranford\\", see Three Tales of Cranford: Cranford, The Cage at Cranford, and The Moorland Cottage. Besides the familiar and beloved cast of the original Cranford such as Miss Matty (Dame Judi Dench), Miss Pole (Imelda Staunton), Mrs Forrester (Julia McKenzie), Miss Tomkinson (Deborah Findlay), etc. several new characters are introduced, such as Lady Glenmire (played by Celia Imrie), and the conjuror Signor Brunoni(Tim Curry).<br /><br />The first part is set in summer 1844 - it has been two years since dear Miss Matty (Dame Judi Dench in another luminous portrayal) lost her beloved sister Deborah, and a year since Sophy Hutton married Dr Harrison (these two characters are no longer in this show). Miss Matty seems content with the presence of her brother Peter (Nicholas Le Prevost) who is home from India, and helps look after Tilly, the baby of her maid Martha and carpenter Jem Hearne. The continuity from the original Cranford is seen in the railway project which still looms menacingly over the town. Things are also made more exciting with the arrival of Mr. Buxton, a wealthy widower who lives with his ward Erminia (Michelle Dockery) and his son William (Tom Hiddleston who is quite the eye candy). Life in Cranford is always full of surprises and when Lady Ludlow's long absent son Septimus (Rory Kinnear) arrives, things take unexpected turns, precipitated by a tragedy in the family. The old tensions are there - especially between those that are against the railroad project and those ,like Captain Brown and young William who feel that modernization is essential to Cranford's long-term survival. Miss Matty, in her usual subtle fashion, gets involved in some of these village proceedings, with some rather startling results.<br /><br />The second part is set later in the year 1844, October up till Christmas - Miss Matty and her friends are predictably excited at the visit of Lady Glenmire (Celia Imrie) but when Mrs Jamieson (Barbara Flynn) feels no one amongst her peers is of suitably high rank to meet her, she and Lady Glenmire get snubbed by Matty and company and it is left to Lady Glenmire to set things right in a most memorable way. Matty also faces some challenges that involves a falling out amongst her circle of friends,a serious romance between William and a young woman deemed unsuitable by his father (which has Matty pondering the wisdom of her 'involvement' in bringing the pair together), and more tragedy on the horizon, affecting the citizens of Cranford.<br /><br />A fair note of warning - this particular installment in the Cranford franchise is much more subdued than the original and there's quite a fair bit of tragedy - there's death (involving a couple of familiar characters who were also in the original), grief, tension, family drama, imperiled friendships, the age-old battle between those opposed to change and those who embrace the challenges of modernization, etc., but there's also romance and lighthearted moments. However, I can understand how this particular follow-up might disappoint purists who loved the original and how well it adhered to Gaskell's novel. Personally, I loved the original Cranford and thought it was a superior production, and though I like RTC, the plot is not as engaging as the original. As for the production qualities - they are excellent. The cinematography is gorgeous, capturing the beauty of the village surroundings as well as the period details, and the score complements the story perfectly.e second part is set later in the year 1844, October up till Christmas - Miss Matty and her friends are predictably excited at the visit of Lady Glenmire (Celia Imrie) but when Mrs Jamieson (Barbara Flynn) feels no one amongst her peers is of suitably high rank to meet her, she and Lady Glenmire get snubbed by Matty and company and it is left to Lady Glenmire to set things right in a most memorable way. B0060I0IG2|5.0|Recently, Beethoven symphony recordings have seemingly returned to modern instruments but carrying strong period influences. Chailly uses the standard Peters scores but shows respect for period performance practices used in Beethoven's time. Konwitschny had similar views and practices and was the first to record this way. Chailly seems like a bloodline successor, with the magnificent sound the players provided for Franz all those years ago, but with today's modern sound-capture raising the proverbial bar for sonics that are staggering to listen to. You have not heard (this) Beethoven previously, and Chailly's view is a revelation. While it is usually a \\"trap\\" Chailly adheres to Beethoven's metronome markings. He makes specific note in the booklet notes regarding this, and they only work if the conductor feels these speeds \\"in his blood\\" so to speak, as if they themselves would have also chosen them.<br /><br />And so, we get what a great many are going to call \\"Rushed\\" interpretations. However, I do not agree, and urge you to listen to these recordings yourself and make your own judgment as I have. Under Chailly's direction Symphony #1 is overwhelmingly convincing that he has gotten it right. This is fierce Beethoven, and it is notable; daring; as you have never heard before. It is also totally fulfilling and it is not to be forgotten nor placed \\"beside\\" the previous recordings.<br /><br />The Second displays great power, again the metronome markings, and this approach Fully justifies itself. All three further movements display this forward drive, and one gets the effect that perhaps Beethoven's style was more mature than we usually think at this stage in his life. Again, this is a notable recording and interpretation, and you will not soon forget it, it's nearly infectious.<br /><br />The Third under Chailly leaves NO doubt that Beethoven had reached his first compositional maturity. This is hard driving, furious Beethoven, determined; visionary and impatient...totally convincing even though it is over a minute faster than Mackerras's outing. The March is steady and measured as it should be, with Chailly holding back from the usual quickening in the forceful sections. Again, Fierce Beethoven....magnificent. The finale is rapid but in rich lush sound, nearly twice as fast as most other performances. But, this is impressive, broad, and grand. Certainly the finest reading of this symphony as I have ever heard. Note that!<br /><br />Number 4 is dramatic and full of contrast, sonically brilliant. Beginning with a marvelously hushed intro it moves into a swifter Adagio etc., and once he has built up this tension, Chailly does not allow it to slacken. Beethoven indicated a minimum 84 marking here, and Chailly strictly holds to it. This is grand Beethoven absolutely convincing under the sure hand of Mr. Chailly. Make no mistake about it, this guy has spent time thinking through this body of work, and it is presented with a surety that will leave you convinced.<br /><br />Number 5 also carries a similar level of ferocity, but Chailly holds back some on the weight here and this allows for some unusual clarity of detail. Very Nice. However, his tempo is unrelenting in its drive and this makes for a fabulous transition from his beautiful slow Andante to a shattering end. Repeats are held in check. The finale is impatient, dramatic, swiftly moving forward to it's climax. No nobility here...it is not required. A convincing offering, again, under the conductor's sure direction/thinking.<br /><br />If I have a problem anywhere with this cycle, it is here in the Sixth, swiftly opening and pushing forward. There is an uncomfortableness here for me. I have come to love the languid opening and the joyfulness usually lavished on this work. The second movement is able to sustain the faster tempo and is beautiful here, but it loses some of its effect as the opening movement was taken at such a similar speed. With the drama of the storm, Chailly isagain in his element. Massive chords where required and fierce drums are supplied admirably. The finale is gracious and stately, as the first movement should have perhaps been. I would be interested in hearing other's thoughts regarding this work.<br /><br />With Number 7, Chailly comes relatively close to Kleiber's Vienna Philharmonic recording. Here the main part of the first movement is superb and magnificently pulled off by Chailly's innate thoughts on \\"how things should go here\\". Poised, full of fire, repeat included (unusual). The finale is again fiercely intense, and fully inspired on the part of Chailly. This again is powerful and well thought out, Beethoven.<br /><br />Eight, again, is forward and rapidly moving. But, listen to it, take it in, and I guarantee you will come away believing this is \\"right\\"! Fury; but controlled fury, again here by Chailly makes for a memorable and vivid Eighth that you will come to love.<br /><br />Slower now, in the Ninth, the first tutti with it's forceful attack, gives us a clear and riveting timpani entrance, cleanly and forcefully cutting through the orchestra. Magnificent! Chailly's pace, again, is unrelenting but the rhythm carries strongly and clearly. The recapitulation, dramatic beyond belief comes through as one would expect as Beethoven demanded that the timpani should play fortissimo throughout. Many conductors are timid here....not Chailly! However, Chailly maintains detail here, and the woodwinds are beautifully allowed to display themselves against the timpani. The finale is excellent and full of excitement, the soloists are first rate, particularly the men, richly captured against the players. The coda is lovely high and clear...beautifully captured.<br /><br />The overtures are quite a lovely bonus and are all wonderfully played. Leonore #3 is forceful and quite direct (this is a good thing, of course) and the trumpets are simply marvelous. The Creatures of Prometheus follows in the same vein under Chailly's direction, well formed by the players. The timpani work in the Coriolan is one of those spots that will give you chills....what a luxury to hear details like this! Egmont here stands out because of the piccolo so clearly and cleanly presented at the finale, not often heard so strikingly. King Stephen and Namensfeier are rather unstriking, but no matter, the balance of the set \\"outweighs\\" them!<br /><br />This set will become the new standard-bearer, I am sure. What is striking is that the Gewandhaus was the first place to play a complete cycle of these symphonies in 1825-26 while the Man still was alive. Now, all these years later, Chailly gives us a new cycle of these works with this treasured group, in their magnificent-sounding-hall, the first of the new millennium....how very fitting. Also, it is fortuitous that Chailly be the man in charge here....again fitting, as for my money, he is \\"the best on offer\\" for our time for this music. You will be delighted and totally satisfied with the acquisition of these striking and magnificent interpretations, beautifully presented in their hardbound book carrier in it's protective slipcase. Chailly is to be congratulated for adhering  to the pattern of not altering his tempo once he has set it.<br /><br />This set is absolutely notable and deserves your undivided attention as soon as possible...you will not regret this time well spent with the great master under the visionary eye of Maestro Chailly.<br /><br />Life is short, folks, enjoy these without delay!<br /><br />~operabruince drums are supplied admirably. The finale is gracious and stately, as the first movement should have perhaps been. I would be interested in hearing other's thoughts regarding this work. B0014E9188|4.0|From strictly a live DVD rating and musical enjoyment perspective, I'd rate this DVD a 4 to a 4.5. Strong 4.5 if there weren't any distracting effects added in post. I prefer a straight forward video recording of the event without effects because it is more true to life. In terms of faithfulness to the CD versions of the songs, I'd rate this DVD closer to 3 or 3.5.<br /><br />First, I realize that the songs are not mine, and that Porcupine Tree has every right to change sounds, omit ambient effects and other parts of songs and to change lyrics for live performances.<br /><br />Second, I own all of the available Porcupine Tree albums available thus far. I like all of them, some more than others. I also own both the Arriving Somewhere and Anesthetize DVD's. I have to say I prefer Anesthetize to Arriving simply because the songs are closer to what I'm used to hearing on the albums. I'm not faulting the musicianship on this DVD, which is excellent as usual, but the lack of specific song elements which were deliberately put in place in the studio for the CD's but omitted during the live performance. Many of these were keyboard driven, which could have been reproduced via samples during the show, so it isn't about not enough musicians to accomplish the task in case that was the thought that came to mind.<br /><br />For me, there are too many key elements present on the CD versions which are missing on the Arriving Somewhere DVD versions of the songs. Perhaps it's the way I listen to songs, but to me, not hearing specific ambient sounds and/or chords detracts from the overall experience. Some of the biggest offenders that come to mind are as follows:<br /><br />The very beginning of Even Less where the girl laughing (as heard on the CD) is missing from the live version. The lyrics are actually changed on Even Less from \\"You're a  martyr for even less\\" to \\"I'm a martyr to even less\\".<br /><br />On Trains there are some distinct changes to the acoustic guitar parts, specifically with the phrases that are reminiscent of Spanish music with hand claps and timing. The claps are there on the DVD via audience participation, but the picking of notes on the CD is replaced with strumming on Steven's part. Perhaps this is due to the line-up changes the band has seen?(This particular piece is what got my brother-in-law to start listening to PT, the Spanish-sounding part specifically as he really liked the way the changed styles mid-song.) The overall timing is pretty fast compared to the CD version, which is different, not bad.<br /><br />I'll stop here with this: The music is good, and the band performs well, but for individuals who listen to CD's and enjoy specific keyboard parts or drum riffs or dissonant guitar chords, etc you may be finding yourself wishing some song components weren't omitted when viewing this DVD.<br /><br />If you don't expect specific elements in a song this DVD will not disappoint. It's a great live performance, it's simply different!

1594203059|5.0|A wonderful book you should add to your reading pile. Ferguson is a Scots historian now at Harvard. This is a terrifically researched exploration of why a few petty squabbling states in Europe, against all odds, came to dominate the world. The author has a great ability to pull the illuminating fact or pertinent quote from the morass of history. He also explores why South America, which was by far the richer set of colonies, is now far poorer than North America. (Hint: widespread property ownership and property rights.) Fergusson says Western Civilization had six \\"killer apps\\" that led them to dominate the world: competition, science, property rights, medicine, the consumer society and the work ethic. He makes a solid case that these six factors not only led to western dominance, but the high standard of living in western society, pursued today by the rest of the world.<br /><br />Some of the bits were worth the price of the book. Martin Luther's defense of publishing the Koran in order that Christians could see \\"how entirely cursed, abominable and desperate a book it is.\\" John Locke's attempt to ban lawyers in Carolina. The author proclaiming the US Constitution \\"the most impressive piece of political institution building in all of history.\\" His note that Tocqueville identified the essential difference between the American and French revolutions, a preference for liberty in ours and equality in theirs (a warning to us today). His insight that the threat to the west comes not from radical Islam, \\"but from our own lack of understanding of, and faith in, our own cultural heritage.\\" He points out that Asians now work far more hours than Americans, and we more than Europeans.<br /><br />That the Chinese Communists party had a report \\"specifying three requirements for sustainable economic growth: property rights as a foundation, the law as a safeguard and morality as a support\\" is telling. And Ferguson's comment that, \\"mass immigration is not necessarily the solvent of a civilization, if the migrants embrace, and are encouraged to embrace, the values of the civilization to which they are moving\\" should inform our immigration debate.<br /><br />And this: \\"\\"It is important to remember that most cases of civilizational collapse are associated with fiscal crisis as well as wars. All of the examples discussed above were preceded by sharp imbalances between revenues and expenditures, as well as by difficulties with financing public debt.\\" Are you listening, Washington? (No, alas.)<br /><br />Ferguson asks if we can maintain western civilization and western dominance. That's an open question. I read the hard copy, but my wife listened to it on disk in the car. Ferguson reads the book himself, but adds in wonderful accents on the quotes. Do yourself a favor and read this book.<br /><br />Robert A. B003TML0VO|3.0|Carlos Santana and his searing guitar lines have been a big part of my life since high school, when a friend turned me on to the first and second LPs from the original incarnation of the Santana Band.  Over the years he's gone various places, from the Indian guru-influenced music of Devadip Carlos Santana trading licks with Mahavishnu John McLaughlin, to the jazz-influenced Borboletta and Welcome (still my two favorite Santana CDs--Tom Coster and the late Richard Kermode on keys were a big part of that sound), back to a renewed encounter with his Roman Catholic roots in Sacred Fire, and then the monster accomplishment that was Supernatural.<br />Here, Carlos and the latest version of his band cover some rock hits from the 60s all the way to the 90s.  And they're covers of tunes that have been covered many times before. I was curious to hear how an iconic musician like Carlos Santana would handle them.<br />My interest was more than that of a casual listener to rock music.  I've played keyboards in various bands since the mid-1960s, and have covered nearly all these tunes myself at one time or another.  I've also covered many Santana songs.  So I have a semi-professional interest in this CD; I give it a qualified endorsement.<br />What does Carlos bring to the table?  Well, mostly his trademark searing guitar lines on top of--and all over--updated versions of these songs, along with some catchy Latin percussion on a few of them.  He (or his producers) have a different vocalist on each tune, and that vocalist has to be strong enough to make an impression when pitted against the Santana guitar.  I'm out of touch with current rock music, and other than Chris Cornell--late of Audioslave--and the great Joe Cocker, I really know nothing about the singers.  But each of them acquits him- or herself well, with the exception of rapper NAS.  And his problem is simply that I abhor rap--I don't listen to it, and therefore I'm totally unqualified to pass judgment on his performance of \\"Back in Black.\\"  But more about that later.<br />The formula works well, contrary to what many of the reviewers say.  The big surprise for me was \\"Little Wing,\\" a bittersweet Hendrix tune.  The inimitable Joe Cocker covers this so well--his whiskey-soaked vocals are a perfect choice.  For my ears, this one song alone was worth the price of the CD.  I've never heard a better cover of this classic Hendrix tune.<br />Chris Cornell, who I think has the best set of pipes in rock music at the moment, handles Led Zeppelin's classic \\"Whole Lotta Love\\" just fine--he gives it a different treatment than did Zep's Robert Plant (a vocalist who in his prime was the greatest that rock has produced--sorry Ian Gillian!), and the bridge is less psychedelic than was the Zep original, but it all works.  The same is true of the Rolling Stone's \\"Can't You Hear Me Knocking.\\"<br />Cream's \\"Sunshine of Your Love\\" was a pleasant surprise.  Rob Thomas does a fine job on the vocals, and the addition of keys and Latin percussion make this an outstanding cover of the classic.  The typical Santana screaming guitar solo works just fine--it's not Eric Clapton; it's Carlos Santana.  And that's more than OK.<br />Unlike some of the reviewers, I was disappointed by the rendition of George Harrison's \\"While My Guitar Gently Weeps.\\"  And what disappointed me was the guitar.  The cut begins with a bittersweet cello introduction from the great Yo-Yo Ma (a master stroke, that!) coupled with a classical/flamenco guitar sound that would have been great all the way through.  But too soon, we lose this and get instead Santana's trademark sound all over--the guitar doesn't gently weep, it screams and rants.  Ultimately, this detracts AND distracts from India.Arie's quiet, evocative vocals.<br />I particularly liked the cover of Deep Purple's chestnut, \\"Smoke on the Water.\\"  I don't know how many of Purple's live CDs I have where the band does this tune to end its live set, and some of them feature Ian Gillian after his voice--sadly--has gone downhill and he just can't cut it.  Jacoby Shaddix (otherwise unknown to me--maybe somebody out there knows something about him) handles the vocals as well as Gillian did in his prime, and Santana's guitar would embarrass Ritchie Blackmore if he heard it.<br />T. Rex's \\"Bang A Gong,\\" one of the first glam rock hits, is handled well.  It's a fun tune, and the Santana Band, along with vocalist Gavin Rossdale, maintain the fun with a Latin beat bubbling in the background.<br />The other tunes are competent, with TWO glaring exceptions.  Thankfully they're adjacent to each other on the CD, so when you come to them, you simply fast forward from cut 5 (\\"Photograph\\") to cut 8 (\\"Smoke on the Water\\"), without having to endure two awful covers:  I refer to AC/DC's \\"Back in Black,\\" here performed with rapper NAS, and the Doors' classic tune, \\"Riders on the Storm.\\"  They are SO bad that I feel I have to vent:<br />I never cared much for AC/DC; I didn't like the screechy vocalist in the band, whose voice reminds me of somebody grating fingernails on a chalkboard.  But surely, having the lyric rapped out is no improvement.  So it's a conjunction of two musical phenomena that I can't stand; the aforesaid AC/DC, and rap.  Taken together, they are awful.  Utterly forgettable.  Your life will be better if you don't ever listen to it.<br />But things get worse!  The awfulness reaches a new level with \\"Riders on the Storm.\\"  As I mentioned, I'm a keyboard player--I've done \\"Riders\\" in every rock band I've played in from 1972 forward, I know it backwards and forwards, and love it like an old friend, even though Jim Morrison's lyrics were suitably creepy, particularly part about the \\"killer on the road/whose brain is squirming like a toad.\\"  The great Ray Manzarek in featured on keys on this cut, but somehow that doesn't save it.  Rather than playing his Fender Rhodes electric piano, he plays some of the riffs on what sounds like a screeching Farfisa or Vox organ.  It doesn't work; you can't take riffs written for the crystalline sound of the Fender Rhodes and paste them on a Farfisa!  But what is worse--sacrilege even--is how Carlos handles the guitar part.  Robbie Krieger, the Doors' guitarist, was a master of understatement and restraint.  His solo on \\"Riders,\\" and his background work when Manzarek takes his justifiably famous Rhodes solo (omitted on this cover), are masterpieces of impressionism, spare, lean, and definitely what this song wants and needs.  But Carlos doesn't understand this--his guitar is all over, like a headstrong bull in a china shop full of delicate, finely cut pieces.  The result is atrocious, something I will never listen to again as long as I live.  This song alone takes the album from 5 stars to 3 stars.  I can't imagine what the producer and arranger Howard Benson was thinking!  It's awful, plain damn awful!<br />But those two abominations aside, this is a good album--not a masterpiece like Welcome or Supernatural, but more or less respectful of the artists whose work Santana and his bandmates choose to cover.  Special kudos should go to drummer Dennis Chambers.  His drums are mixed front and center, and they add to the drive and pulse of this music.  He's the glue that holds it all together.<br />So, 3 stars.  I wanted to give it more, but there's just enough awfulness to drag the rating down.  You won't miss anything special if you pass this album by, but if like me you grew up listening to a lot of this music and want to hear a fresh take on it, you may well enjoy Guitar Heaven.  Just skip cuts 6 and 7--You have been warned!gone downhill and he just can't cut it.  Jacoby Shaddix (otherwise unknown to me--maybe somebody out there knows something about him) handles the vocals as well as Gillian did in his prime, and Santana's guitar would embarrass Ritchie Blackmore if he heard it. A lot of bands of the 60's generation,even the Fab Four themselves were breaking up or about to around this time. And this,in a way could've been the end of the Rolling Stones. That is,if Mick Taylor hadn't been bought in perhaps. Not only did he of course bring his own instrumental flavors to the band. But the Stones,resilliant as always found a new way to play the rock n soul they made famous over the years. And it was clear with this album the Stones were'nt going to end here and now. An interpretation of Robert Johnson's \\"Love In Vain\\",\\"Country Honk\\" (an early take on what would become \\"Honky Tonk Woman\\" of course) and \\"Midnight Rambler\\" all showcase a more eerily subdued country-blues flavor than anything I've heard the Stones do before this. The hook is often seen as represented the double edged sword of the countercultural experience. Sort of their answer to the Beatles \\"The End\\" maybe,with a bit of witty resignation. Truthfully, I was a little disappointed once I saw that it was just as much about a personal, internal journey as it was about the literal journey.  But I wasn't disappointed for long.  Cheryl Strayed is a gifted writer, and I was involved in her story immediately.  She decided to solo-hike a large portion of the PCT almost on a whim, and is miserably unprepared for the reality of the undertaking.  The book intersperses physical trail challenges (injuries, wild animals, scary terrain crossings, etc) with self-reflection on her messed-up life to that point.  When she describes her experiences on the PCT, she doesn't spend a lot of time describing the beauty all around her.  Instead, it is about the emotions she experienced on the trail, how the trail affected her, and how the distance from her \\"real life\\" gave her the courage to grow up.  And it's very powerful stuff.

B001EBWITA|3.0|As an on-again, off-again follower of the Vertigo comic this is based upon, I was all geared up to hate it mightily before it had even spun up in the DVD player. With that said, it fares somewhat better than I'd expected. It isn't a raging success story, but it's not a tragic insult to the rich, lengthy source material either. Keanu Reeves is a horrible choice for the lead, no doubt about that - where the funny book Constantine is a complex, witty, bitter but charismatic subculture hero, Keanu's interpretation is as nuanced as a bag of bricks. I don't know how much I can fault the actor for that - after all, he's been playing dress-up with the same character for two decades now - but regardless of where the blame lies, he lacks the unspoken authenticity that role needs to get off the ground. Fortunately, the blow is softened somewhat by a dynamite intro sequence that may as well have been ripped straight from the pages of the comic and a decent, if indulgent, central storyline. With a full-blown war between heaven and hell at its core, it would've been very easy to get overly adventurous with the film's scope, but it wisely maintains a short focal range and that keeps things mostly grounded. It won't be finding a home on anyone's top ten list, and it's not easy to look past Keanu being Keanu (with Shia freaking LaBeouf crammed in as a throwaway sidekick), but the tone is right and the special effects work is a grand success. Not nearly what it could've been, but also not what I feared it would become.

After reading all the Amazon reviews I was totally confused so I went to a couple of brick and mortar stores to look at what was available in person.  I chose the Belkin because it fit the Kindle without a lot of elastic overlap, felt nice in my hands (like an old bound leather book), folded back nicely and closed easily. I'm sure there are other covers that are good, but I'm very pleased and happy I bought this one.
